## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: thermomechanics
**Analysis**: This task involves modeling stress as a function of strain and temperature, which is a common scenario in thermomechanics, particularly in the study of material behavior under varying temperature conditions.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Linear elasticity: Stress = c0 * Young's modulus * Strain (isotropic material behavior)
- Ramberg-Osgood model: Stress = c0 * (Strain + c1 * Strain^c2) (nonlinear material behavior)
- Hyperelastic material model: Stress = c0 * (J**(-2/3) - 1) (deviatoric stress in incompressible materials)
- Saint-Venant-Kirchhoff material model: Stress = c0 * Young's modulus * (1 + c1 * Strain) (anisotropic material behavior)
- Navier's equation: Stress = c0 * (2 * nu * Strain + (1 - 2 * nu) * (x + y) * Strain) (plane stress problem)
- Mooney-Rivlin model: Stress = c0 * (I1 - 3) + c1 * (I2 - 3) (incompressible rubber-like materials)
- Temperature-dependent material model: Stress = c0 * (Strain) / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 / T)) (temperature-dependent material behavior)
- Thermal expansion: Stress = c0 * Alpha * T (thermal stress due to expansion)
- Von Mises yield criterion: Stress = c0 * sqrt((Strain_x**2 + Strain_y**2 + Strain_z**2) / 2) (yielding of ductile materials)
- Barlat's yield criterion: Stress = c0 * (sqrt((Strain_x**2 + Strain_y**2 + Strain_z**2) / 2) + c1 * (Abs(Strain_x) + Abs(Strain_y) + Abs(Strain_z)) / 2) (yielding of anisotropic materials)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Stress-strain nonlinearity → power-law terms or nonlinear functions such as tanh, exp(c*epsilon)
- Temperature-dependent stress → temperature-dependent scaling (c0*T), or rational functions of T
- Anisotropic material behavior → multiplicative strain dependence, or use of multiple material constants
- Thermal expansion effects → add thermal expansion term (c0*Alpha*T) to stress model
- Strain-hardening behavior → add strain-dependent term (c0*(1 + c1*epsilon)) to stress model
- Material degradation → add multiplicative factor (c0*exp(c1*epsilon)) to stress model
- Reducing symmetry → use of multiple material constants, or use of symmetry-breaking terms
- Stress-strain decoupling → use of different material constants for different strain components
- Simple cubic nonlinearity → tanh term, or use of parametric functions such as sigmoid
- Strain-dependent thermal expansion → add temperature-dependent term (c0*exp(c1*epsilon)) to thermal expansion model
- Coupled thermomechanics → use of coupled differential equations for temperature and stress evolution
